### Post by salttalk on 2006-05-08
Hi. I'm a newbie and I know almost nothing about computers.

In the 80's, I was a computer programmer for a year. Then I became a tech writer for a few years. Yet, during the past 15 years or so, it's been amazing how much I have managed carefully to forget, and how far I have fallen behind the annoying advances in technology.

(OK, I think computer-industry years are something like double "dog" years, almost 14 per human year. So about 15 human years outside the industry would be nearly 210 computer-industry years.)

Anyway, quite a few months ago I received a free used computer from a friend in my church, to replace my even older computer. However, his computer had a Dutch Windows operating system. And I only speak English.

Thus, I either had to learn Dutch real quick, or get a new operating system. Since money was no object (there was not a single object in my whole house which could be called "money"), I decided to try Ubuntu Linux (one of the free OSs recommended by a techy friend).

At first, I was a little reluctant to try Linux, because of the juicy UNIX horror stories which battle-hardened techy soldiers used to embellish upon for their personal glory, back in the 80's, and due to my total ignorance of it. (I always felt inferior to those guys who could play around with UNIX.)

But I had to get that computer working, one way or another. Our family relies heavily on computers, because three out of four of us have neurological disabilities. (Computers are a real God-send to disabled souls, since they make communication and education much easier -- keyboarding uses two cognitive functions of the brain, while handwriting uses six.) Therefore, in desperation, I tried Ubuntu.

Actually, my apprehension was not entirely in vain. Sometimes I have spent days trying to get sound, or something else, to work on this Linux system. But I could have made the same things run in half an hour on Windows. After some months, I still can't get some things to run. Alas, my old scanner sadly sits idly by, on the table beside me, poor lonely thing. (I never used it much anyway!)

Still, with the help of this forum, and through the many other help files available (along with dear, sweet, gorgeous, lovable "Automatix," whom I may now call "my precious") I have been able to get Ubuntu to function very well, to do almost all my work as a writer (free Christian materials, educational and persuasive non-fiction). Of course, I must admit, to my shame, that I use my sons' Windows computers to do a few things -- but hardly ever.

Most of all, I love the attitude of Linux. Just imagine the hundreds of thousands of free man-power hours that went into a system like this! Literally thousands of people must have cooperated to produce this beautiful and functional system, along with the extensive support for this system. And the benefits are enormous for those who need free systems (like the disabled, or Christian workers without much of a budget).

Allow me to add this comment too: this system is almost a perfect example of the true biblical principles of cooperation in operation (although it would probably urk most of the money-grubbing false preachers on TV -- about whom I could grumble for hours, but will spare you my lengthy, severely negative diatribes).

Fortunately, I won't get too religious on you.

Nevertheless, apart from all my ramblings, I would just like to say a big THANK YOU to all who have worked so hard on this system and forum. Permit me to even say, "God bless you." Your efforts are very much appreciated!!! You have helped many people.

Greg.

---

### Post by nudnik on 2006-05-09
Regarding SATA support; my drive is allegedly SATA II and it works better with Ubuntu by far than it does with Windows. There is such a contrast between the two OS when it comes to SATA in fact, that I suspect Windows XP does not adequetly support SATA under certain hardware configurations. Hopefully this will be corrected with the release of Vista, as there are certain things for which I continue to use Windows, such as music and video playback. 

 To date, all of my tests show that IDE support is about the same. 

Nvidia driver support is much better under Windows as is audio quality if one is using HD audio. Security and data integrity are far superior with Ubuntu, however, making it indisposable.

### Post by RAV TUX on 2006-05-12
[quote=aysiu] You're probably better off with Mepis or Linspire.

 Blag and Mepis have some pretty good out-of-the-box proprietary formats support.


I know this generally sounds like a downer. I say use Mepis. [/quote] 

Nice intro. but I have too strongly disagree with the Mepis plug, I found Mepis is NOT a good distro for newbies.

I have tried many distros and just tried Mepis the splash screens while pretty are not what a newbie needs.

Mepis may be good for an intermediate user...but I have burned many iso images of distros and I as a newbie put every Linux Distro through an acid test.

The first is ease of navigation of the distro website, the second is ease of loading OS without stumbling blocks. The third of course is ease of use if the distro makes it that far.

Thus far Mepis has failed on both of the first two acid test. Ease of navigation: NOT easy; then loaded OS: (again while I had high hopes) NOT easy.

The solution is probably easy but I am not that inspired to use it now and have already moved on to the next distro "Fedora Core 5"

For newbies like myself I would first and foremost suggest Ubuntu and if you need help post for help here in the forum.

Now if like the premise of post #1, would be to suggest an easier alternative I would have to support a couple that I found great ease and success with:

1. Yoper...not just one of the easiest but also one of the most refined, elegant and intelligent distros. loading and partitioning is smooth, easy, and again intelligent.(KDE)
[http://www.yoper.com/]("http://www.yoper.com/")

2. PC-BSD...one of the quickest of all OS builds to install, smooth and uneventful. The KDE environment is knock your socks off sexy and fun and the downloads of software easy and simple from their website.
[http://www.pcbsd.org/]("http://www.pcbsd.org/")

3. Morphix/Gnome(combined heavy GUI 0.5pre4)...while this is still only good for the live CD. It is a wonderful and easy distro in development, the above two are great if you want KDE, this is a beautiful distro for Gnome. Again this is only a live CD(you can install but it is still too new). I say this is good for a Linux newbie who may have a EM64T computer because most are hard to get to run on advanced hardware including Ubuntu.
[http://www.morphix.org/]("http://www.morphix.org/")

4. Dreamlinux Studio Edition...another Morphix based live CD, excellent if you are into multimedia, music, video production. Simply awesome.
[http://www.dreamlinux.com.br/english/index.html]("http://www.dreamlinux.com.br/english/index.html")



Now my Ubuntu story, around last February 2005 my computer died. A friend gave me an old Windows 98SE, old Compaq. Her nephew had loaded Kazaa without a firewall for music. When I got it it had over 11,000 viruses on it. It would literally crash every 2 minutes. I managed to clean it up and get it running for about 20 minutes. Then our pet bunny chewed through the mouse cord and it wouldn't recognize the new USB mouse. Then it stopped recognizing my Internet connection.

So I had an old computer that would crash every 20 minutes, NO mouse, and NO Internet connection. needless to say I had, had it with windows. So I begin to research Linux. My first choice was SUSE, until I found out about Ubuntu. The initial reviews were a little scary because it was billed as being more technical but with more customization. The free with free shipping was a motivator as was the Ubuntu philosophy. So I ordered 100 CD's: 60 for PC, 20 for 64bit, and 20 for Apple. I figured if I was getting a free OS the least I could do is distribute for Ubuntu.

So I finally got the first delivery in of the H.Hedgehog...I put the CD in restarted the computer, nothing. Then I read the directions, completely reboot, so I disconnected the power and powered back up. It started, how exciting! It recognized the mouse and the Internet connection (DSL) and it was lightening fast! Ubuntu hasn't crashed yet!  I got a new shipment of 100 Breezy Badgers, and I am now using Dapper Beta, since I just recently learned how to burn iso images. I happily distribute Ubuntu to friends, family, business associates, even customers(I am not in the computer industry), I even got my friend who happens to be my boss to load Ubuntu as a dual boot on his Fujitsu convertible Tablet.

My story is a success for Ubuntu and in contrast to the downers you may hear, very positive. While everyone has different stories based on their hardware or experience. Remember my technical computer experience was none and my hardware crap when I started and I had success.

Since then I bought an Intel EM64T dual-core with all the latest sound card and everything else...the problem is I can't get Ubuntu to load on it. Not even the live CD. I have had plenty of help here but no luck. I will continue to try. I look forward to the official new release.
:mrgreen:

To Aysiu great work overall on your hard work and I completely enjoy your inspiration and tack fullness.



























.

In a sea of Linux Zealots and people who hate Microsoft for whatever reasons, I'm incredibly greatful that this community has resources like this to prime potential newcomers for headaches and benefits they stand to gain.

### Post by bigkahoona on 2006-05-26
I am one of the "tough cases", having worked (and still am!) as a Windows Administrator and having used Windows since 3.11, so everything *nixy pretty much gave me a hard time when I first started, but I knew that before starting and so I didn't let it get me down.
The only advantage when starting to use any Linux Distro after having excessively used Windows for 15 years is that you aren't really afraid of breaking anything as you've seen a lot of things broken many times before. ;) 

So anyways, I installed SuSe 9.2 last year and still have it installed as a secondary OS sitting next to Windows XP on one of my notebooks.

I have now installed Dapper Drake Beta 2 on another notebook and I must say I am thoroughly impressed! I couldn't believe it when even my PCMCIA WLAN card was recognized and worked on install without additional driver installation or workarounds. Try that in Windows! Ok, I bought it with Linux support in mind (went for an Atheros chipset), but still.
I am now upgrading this notebook's memory, because it's going to get a Windows XP install - but as a Virtual Machine in Ubuntu only. :-D 

After having looked at Linux a few years back and then again recently, I must say the dev community has come a long way to where we are now with regards to desktop deployment. The amount of hardware that works "out-of-the-box" is truly impressive.

I think the analogy of learning a new language to getting used to Linux after using Windows for a long time is a really good one.
That's why I decided that my son (6 yo.), who has just gotten his first computer, will grow up "bilingual". I have installed Edubuntu as the main OS and a bare XP system (without programs, just sound/graphics drivers and the mandatory F-Secure AV), in case he wants to play games. That way he learns about both Linux and Windows and can later decide for himself what he favors (I am willing to place a bet on Linux, actually).

Personally, I think SuSe is good (I am not a big fan of .RPMs), but Ubuntu just ROCKS HARD!

At work I am forced to sysadmin Windows systems, at home it's become only a secondary OS.
Thanks to everyone dedicating their time to develop and maintain the Linux Kernel and to those who gave us Ubuntu!

[SIZE="4"]Ubuntu is ***definitely*** for me.[/SIZE]

*PS: The wife's been pestering me for a while now: "So, when are you going to install this Linux on my Notebook??"
This weekend, Darling, I promise! :-D

### Post by praxxus on 2006-05-26
So far... I'd have to say it's looking very promising :) 

I've been dual-booting between Windows XP and Suse for some time now, but after moving and having to install a wireless card for internet access my Suse install hasn't been of much use. Been fighting it on and off for weeks trying to get it to cooperate with my Linksys card with no luck. So on to Ubuntu..... downloaded the latest Dapper beta and gave it a whirl. Look at that.... native support for the card right off the bat! (Ralink RT2500 chipset for those intested in such details)
Gnome desktop takes a little getting used to after being in KDE for so long but I think I'll make it. 

Building and repairing pc's for friends and family has been a hobby/side business for almost 20 years now, so command line interfaces are an old friend and quite often a faster way of getting some tasks accomplished. Windows is still firmly entrenched on my wifes machine and on my own (mainly for games and some video processing for now) but eventually I will have my machine converted over completely. I've never been one to accept that things "had" to be done a certain way and the level of control linux in general provides has always been the main attractant for me.

Ubuntu performs very quickly and smoothly on my pc, snappier than Suse which I really had no complaints about aside from the wireless issues.
The Ubuntu community seems very friendly and helpful from what I've read so far... hopefully I can contribute somewhat myself as I learn more.

*edit*
Now my mp3's play... my dvd's play.... Ubuntu is looking better all the time. Going to drive my wife nuts when I finally switch over completely :)

### Post by ushaba on 2006-06-06
i like it the most of all the distros i've tried but elive (but that was extremely unstable), especially apt-get beauty, but there are quite a few problems. on this laptop, at least, changing kernels, xorg.conf hand-editing, skype problems, etc, all have to be completed immediately before i even think about doing anything else after installing. i won't give up ubuntu because i love the forums, apt-get, and a few others things, but it IS frustrating. luckily, that level of frustration and sense of utter confusion is what brought me to linux in the first place. in windows, if you search for any problem, pretty much any problem at all on google, you won't get useful results. you'll get ads for software to fix your problems, advice to "make sure the computer is turned on" etc. i have no idea how people ever learn anything about computers with a windows box, given all the weird crap aimed at the willingly computer illiterate user. in this way, linux is liberating for a curious user who really does want to know how everything works.  granted, right now, very little is working right over here, but i'm learning a whole lot about the system architecture, my hardware, how my box connects to the internet, ping times, etc. it'd be nice if it "just worked," as far as those few things that keep killing me:skype, scim, wireless, xorg.conf and the like, but if that were the case, i'd probably lose the joy of exploration and just get distracted playing games like on windows.

i sure wish that using linux is just a matter of choice, for linux is my personal choice (breezy did run fast enough in an old P3). problem is i am assigned to make our offcie compliant with BSA requirments. i've encourage the divisions who have money to buy XP but most can't affrod it. then our director heard about Linux, that its free, and told us to switch. 

heck, it is easire seling ice cream to eskimos than Linux to my fellows who grew up on nothing but Windows. Linux still has to shed its geeky reputation and shutters just close when it is mentioned here. They even got closed further when i can't make a lot of our printers and other peripherals run in ubuntu (they are jsut not supported). Linux still has a lot to go before it becomes mainstream and geeks (or the geekiest of geeks) does not care that much because Linux already wroks for them. People like me don't ask for Linux to become like Windows (what's the use?), we want it to provide us a better computing experience than Windows, even for the most un-geekiest of computer users.

AND WE DO APPRECIATE YOUR WORK (you geeks and geekiest of geeks out there).

To me, Ubuntu Linux is ready for the mainstream, as long as people don't expect it to be like Windows. I'm a Linux newbie. But on the whole I think it runs a lot better than XP did/does and has a lot more to offer than XP did/does. When you know how to do things they're generally easier to do than in Windows.

One thing at a time, but I'm no geek, and I use Ubuntu for 90% of my computing, so if that's not mainstream, what is?

### Post by fridayxiii on 2006-06-16
My answer has to be a resounding enthusiastic YES.  

I'm not a Microsoft or Windows hater, but admittedly my PC experience has grown stale.  At home I login to XP, do my thing, yada yada yada, log out.  It was almost an unpleasant utilitarian existence: do what must be done & get out ASAP.  I've definitely been looking for someting different, but cannot afford Mac, seemingly enslaved to Windows forever.  Then, from a Digg article comes the revelation: Ubuntu...

I registered as a member of the forums & read aysiu's article (and was subsequently hooked) before even installing the OS.  The challenge of assimilating & hopefully taming a totally new OS - having new concepts & commands to learn, new applications to play with & a new environment in which to do it - is absolutely invigorating.  Having a totally free, open-source OS that's indeed different, more stable & secure (still cannot get over the lack of need for anti-virus or a firewall), learning along the way & having an unparalleled user community to pry help from has renewed my interest in computing again.  I had a hard time shutting down last night & a harder time leaving Linux behind for work this morning!  

The only downside is that my fiancee will be fighting me for time on the PC again - at least until I step away from dual boot & get a dedicated Linux box, leaving XP to her.  =D>  I foresee a day in the none-too-distant future that I become a total Linux convert, and will be happy to do so.  Yes, Ubuntu is absolutely positively 100% for me. :mrgreen:

[QUOTE=aysiu]Not Windows
Do you want an OS that's Windows without the spyware? Linux isn't for you. Linux is not Windows. Linux can perform tasks that Windows can perform and even more, but it is not Windows. If you can't come with an open mind to Linux, then don't come at all. It's kind of like going from Windows to Mac. There's a learning curve. If you don't have time to learn, just cough up some money for anti-virus and learn more about securing your Windows computer. I've done it. It's not that hard.

I know this generally sounds like a downer. I say use Mepis. I say use Windows. I say something may not work, or you may have to do a lot of copying and pasting to get something working. ### Post by 3rdalbum on 2006-07-10
> **charlier653 said:**
> 

one question i have is defrag.....is it something that is needed as under windows? if so, can someone point me in the right direction for learning how to do this?


You'll be happy to find out that Ubuntu's default filesystem format, ext3, is resistant to fragmentation. I think pretty much all the filesystems that Linux can be run from are all fragmentation-resistant.

Somebody did write a script that defrags your partition, but it was experimental and not really necessary anyway.

### Post by aysiu on 2006-07-11
From [Wikipedia](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ext3): > There is no ext3 defragmentation tool. An offline ext2 defragmenter, e2defrag, exists but requires that the ext3 filesystem be converted back to ext2 first. However, defragmentation has long been considered a non-issue for ext2/ext3, since they are much better at placing files on the disk versus old FAT-based filesystems.

Unlike ext2, ext3 zeroes out the block pointers in the inodes of deleted files. Because this removes all metadata for the affected files, the files cannot be recovered directly. The user's only recourse is to grep the hard drive for data known to signal the start and end of the file. This provides slightly more secure deletion than ext2, which can be either an advantage or a disadvantage.

---

### Post by Ummagumma on 2006-07-12
Hmm, is Linux for me ?

I guess not.

After my first experience with Ubuntu 5.10 I got it off my harddrive and invested in XP.

The reasons were:

- looooong time it took to cut & paste commands into terminal window where I felt a doubleclick shoudl've been all it takes

- no shockwave support, and flash or other formats working only after a lot (hours !) of tweaking, and even then not very reliably

- choppy DVD playback

- no support for Pocket PC PDA (yes there's Synce but I couldn't make it work no matter what)

- no support for my Visioneer scanner - that alone made monetary advantage irrelevant as a new scanner would cost me as much as XP Home did

- most irritating of all the rotten attitude on part of many Linux zealots when I asked questions on the usenet - calling me a liar and a wintroll because I said something didn't work, lecturing me on a "proper" way to ask a question, calling me an idiot because I didn't RTFM first, etc. Not a type of behavior I was used to when asking OS related questions before. 

OTOH, in XP everything just worked, with no tweaking. Sorry, I know it's supposed to be shitty and virus ridden, but it wasn't for me. Spybot + AVG + router pretty much take care of spyware and malware with little if any intervention from me.

But guess what - I'm back to having Ubuntu on my 2nd drive. There's lots of things that I still like about it - I think it has a cleaner interface, I like the visual style, I like to explore the possibilities. I installed it on my inlaws computer (they had an old box with Win ME on it) and they like it, too. It's definitely a worthwile project, I'm just not sure it's there yet. If anything, it reminds me of W95 / 98. My own personal opinion.

### Post by Brunellus on 2006-07-13

Usenet gets a bad rep around here, and I'm going to step up and defend it.

There are some usenet communities that are very helpful/supportive.  This is great.

For everything else, there are killfiles/scorefiles.  You have to moderate yourself and everyone else on usenet.  In a way, this is preferable (to me at least) to having arbitrary moderators.

My home desktop is a Windows XP MCE 2005 machine and I'm going cold-turkey Ubuntu on my notebook (X Window threw several errors and refused to function on my desktop) for the next 30 days in the hopes that I can provide a more objective record of experience on it to some of my acquiantances who are may be on the fence.  By far I've found Ubuntu to be the best desktop iteration of a Linux distrobution ever.  So far the only problems I have found is the lack of clear documentation to get the user started which tends to become an intimidation factor.  Mind you, I'm no novice.  I've been a bash user for as long as I can remember, I've simply never used Linux as a desktop solution.

As communities go, I have to say that Ubuntu is a refreshing change from other Linux communities I've come across.  Most of you are very friendly, and helpful. All of this has weighed in heavily in the decision that I've come to to support Ubuntu even if only on a minor level. Either way I intend to stick around here and simply hope you will accept the support I can give to you as a community and distrobution.

### Post by 3rdalbum on 2006-07-16
> **robmit said:**
> 
If someone is not looking for an operating system but instead is looking for a "solution" that simply allows them to access the web, work with documents, spreadsheets, etc..., and use email would you recommend Linux to them?  If so, do you believe this person can keep their system maintained and up to date without becoming an advanced Linux user?

Definately and definately. I access the web, work with documents, print them out, use e-mail, and play music without doing anything my barely-Windows-literate boss couldn't figure out. As for keeping the system maintained and up-to-date, does it matter if the system is old as long as the work can get done? Even then, all you've got to do to update the system is right-click on an icon and select "Install All Updates" from the menu, then type your password.

EDIT: Linux systems don't require defragmentation, virus scans, or user-initiated disk scans, so the only maintanance involved is of one's own home directory.

My father hasn't touched a command-line since 1990, and I once found him trying out Inkscape on Ubuntu.

### Post by fatsheep on 2006-07-17
Ubuntu is the first distro of linux I've ever used. Some things about it I love and others I hate. 

**What I like about Ubuntu:**

[LIST=1]
[*]Graphically Ubuntu is *much* better then windows, the desktop, menu, and program interfaces are beautiful.
[*]You can download, install, and uninstall a huge variety of applications in the add/remove list. This is a very convienant feature.
[*]I feel more in control with Ubuntu then with windows. I can go into preferences or administration and configure and tweak to my heart's content.
[*]Everything in Ubuntu is well organized, I have multiple workspaces, applications in one menu, places in the other menu, and system tasks in the other menu. I can drag desktop shortcuts onto the top bar, I can click the buttom left button to hide all windows and the power off, date, and sound are in the top right.
[*]Firefox, Gimp, Open Office, and a lot of other useful programs come preinstalled.
[*]The community is GREAT. A lot of my questions were answered very quickly on the forum and members were very patient with me.
[/LIST]

**What I hate:**

[LIST=1]
[*]Installation was a pain since there weren't any good guides that I could find (initially) on installing it. My first installation attempt failed because I burned the .iso using the Windows CD writer. My second installation attempt failed because I had used the incorrect settings on the CD burner. My third attempt failed because the installer froze when partitioning the hard drive. The fourth attempt was identical to the third. AND FINALLY IT INSTALLED.
[*]Reliance on the command line (terminal). I hate having to use the terminal to do common tasks like installations. I don't mind using it for some things but Ubuntu depends on it WAY to much. I want to be able to install things by just pushing "next" most of the time.
[*]If the program I am looking for isn't in the Add/remove utility's list, I usually have to put a lot of time and effort into installing it (from the command line).
[*]On default settings, Ubuntu comes with tons of fonts but none of the core microsoft web fonts (arial, verdana, georgia, etc...).  Installing them isn't very hard but it's not straight forward either.
[*]You have to tweak the repositories and such to get Sun Java 5.0 installed. Just about all computer users are going to want Java on their system so the needed repositories should be enabled by default. What's worse is the document about installing Java was wrong so I had to spend some time figuring it out.
[*]When I open some full screen games Ubuntu freezes. 
[*]Installing Nvidia Drivers is a pain, I still haven't figured out how to do this.
[/LIST]

I'm not sure if I am going stay with Ubuntu or not, there's so much I like and so much I don't. :(

---

### Post by 3rdalbum on 2006-07-18
> **fatsheep said:**
> 
If the program I am looking for isn't in the Add/remove utility's list, I usually have to put a lot of time and effort into installing it (from the command line).


It will get easier. My first attempts at compiling were useless, but I got better at it quickly. Don't forget that you can often get unofficially-built .debs and .rpms for a whole heap of programs. Also, as Ubuntu is pretty popular now, a lot of the more common things (like WINE) have 3rd-party repositories that you can use.

> **fatsheep said:**
> 
When I open some full screen games Ubuntu freezes. 
Installing Nvidia Drivers is a pain, I still haven't figured out how to do this.

Your gaming problem might be related to the lack of Nvidia drivers. I had lots of trouble playing full-screen games before I installed the ATI drivers.

### Post by AndrewCaul on 2006-07-18
You don't really need to use the command line to install stuff. You can use Synaptic. Most guides use the command line because it's easier to copy a command than tell you what to click.

### Post by 3rdalbum on 2006-07-21
You don't have to install Ubuntu straight off. By booting the "Desktop CD", you can try the system out and make sure that everything is detected correctly. If you have problems, you can just take the CD out, restart back into Windows, and ask away!

If you get everything going in the live environment, you can just run the installer program, then "Manually edit partition table", and then when you restart you'll be able to choose between Windows and Ubuntu. For various reasons, I recommend keeping Windows until you know you don't need it anymore.

The help on this site is very good for people of all computing abilities. Many people on here have been using Ubuntu for a short period of time - short enough to still speak the language of the new user, but long enough to know what they are doing.

Enjoy your adventure.

---

### Post by srunni on 2006-07-22
There's a few things I want to say about this if-2-or-more-pieces-of-hardware-don't-work-don't-use-it thing:
screen resolution is one of the easiest problems to fix, once you know how. there should be a sticky on the method, because it's so easy that if guided, an average computer user can easily solve the problem. Inserting "1280x1024" in a few places in xorg.conf isn't exactly that hard is it?

Also, dual boot is a great way to get around the switching problem, so if more than 2 pieces of hardware don't work, you can just work on it over time - no rush at all, then fully switch when you're ready. Me, I prefer VMWare for this figure-out phase, but either way works. It took months for me to get my graphics card driver fixed, but now I've got it. It took weeks to find the solution to the screen resolution problem, but seconds to implement it. Just today I managed to get Flash support in Firefox.

Maybe if we made a sticky here that listed common problems and how to fix them, things would be a lot more painless.

### Post by aysiu on 2006-07-22
I understand what you're saying, but I'm leaving it in.

It's true that Windows dial-up users still have to download Windows updates and such, but for installing programs in Ubuntu, you almost always have to do it through your own connection.

In Windows, you can buy CDs or DVDs with setup.exe files on them (dial-up won't be needed), or you can download the setup.exe files on someone else's computer.

Downloading .deb files and all their dependencies on another computer is a bit of a hassle.

After nearly a month of use I've decided to keep Ubuntu on my notebook PC.  Very few distrobutions have made me willing to make such a manuever and, in the end, I'm sure it will probably cause me headaches as my work demands .NET, however I'm hoping I can work around this problem with VMs and a little creative thinking.  I'm still unable to use Ubuntu at home as the only distro that wants to work with my desktop is Gentoo.

Let's just say Gentoo and I have met in battle before.

On another note, I'm weighing the possibility of switching from RedHat to Ubuntu for my servers.  While I appreciate the stability and range of applications compatible with RedHat, I'm annoyed by the fact that RHN seems to hate me (a form of hatred that has spawned in spite of the fact that the co-located boxes I have have license subscriptions.)  

I'm interested in seeing where this distrobution goes.  RedHat seemed to be the one to bring Linux to the main stream.  I had friends working in development for Mandrake and felt it was a good transitional distrobution.  Nothing's really come close to what Ubuntu is doing though.  Half of what makes Ubuntu good isn't the quality of the software, the method of electronic distribution or even the user interface.  What makes Ubuntu stand out for me is that steps are clearly made to make the Ubuntu a more approachable Linux.  Simply go to ubuntu.com.  The main page is friendly, explains it's purpose and leads you down paths for whatever solution you want.  Most distrobutions don't seem to go that route.  Most of them have the unmistakable smell of geek on them.

I'm not saying, by any means, that Ubuntu is ready to change the world.  I've no delusions that there's a mass Linux exodus around the corner despite what anyone thinks of the upcoming Vista release or the cries of foul against Microsoft.  There's a lot there.  Simply telling someone what you have is better will not convince them.  They want substance and Linux isn't there yet.  But every year it's making strides.  Ubuntu could be the mentality that Linux really needs to push it along.

I'm going too long here.  My purpose was neither to say Ubuntu was for me or that it wasn't.  I have my complaints about it, but it's good enough for what I can currently run it on.  If anyone feels like they could take the time to help me with the problems I've been having on my desktop, I'd welcome the assistance, but I'm, unfortunately, I bit too proud of my abilities to jump into another forum and ask for help (that and my desktop is the last stronghold I have for my .NET work.)  I hope to spend a little more time here.  I'd like to get involved in the community a little more despite extreme time constraints (I work in defense and, frankly, they don't let their geeks out much.)

There's my soliloquy.  I hope to converse with several of you.  Maybe put some of my coding skills to good use (Linux needs games.)  Catch you all in the forums.

---

### Post by rsk on 2006-08-03
For all the beginners out there comming from another platform (mostly Windows) I've put together a visual guide to help explain Ubuntu in a little more basic termonoligy. Tried to point out where words and things on Linux exist that don't exist on Windows. The guide also ends with the most important tips (EasyUbuntu/Automatix) for getting a lot of things working on Ubuntu that are not simple drop-in so you can sit down and actually start using the desktop as a replacement for Windows with little to no hassle.

If you see a tip I missed please let me know, I'll add it:
[http://www.breakitdownblog.com/2006/08/03/a-manual-for-the-ubuntu-linux-beginner/](http://www.breakitdownblog.com/2006/08/03/a-manual-for-the-ubuntu-linux-beginner/)

I actually wrote this because a friend was asking me tons of questions about Ubuntu. I wanted to get 80-90% of all the started information necessary to get him off his feet, hope it's there for others as well.

### Post by bluntu on 2006-08-06
**LINUX IS FOR YOU IF**:

1: You are smart and can figure things on your own with less texts.
2: Have a lot of patience and **like READING**.
3: Will not give up and look for alternatives like WINE if the software you want to use is not available for Linux.
4: Not afraid of breaking things.
5: You can handle technical stuff.
6: You're not into the eye candies and illusions (Windows is full of it).
7: You want to know how the system operates and to surf the net safely.
8: You don't have a lot of money.
9: You support the Open Source movement and want to be part of it.


**LINUX IS NOT FOR YOU IF**:

1: You don't like technical stuff.
2: You don't like READING.
3: You don't like command-line.
4: You don't have patience and get really frustated easily if things don't work out the first time.
5: You like eye candies and illusions (like Windows).

---

Again, I want to be honest by saying that if you don't like command-line and if you don't have the time to try to understand LINUX structure then you're going to have a hard time using LINUX (does not matter what distro). [http://www.linuxtutorials.org/](http://www.linuxtutorials.org/)

ALSO, LINUX is for YOU if you have nephews, brothers/sisters who uses computer to surf the net and nothing more.

It's called Illusions and I happen to spot a post somewhere on this forum where a member says that he prefers Windows because OpenOffice looks nicer there. There are even people who won't use a software UNLESS the icon of that software is NICE and GOOD looking (I'm exaggerating a bit here).

People who are attaches to these things and the softwares that they like will have a hard time switching to Linux because they will expect the software to run/look BETTER under Linux or else. I was one of these people before but now I have learnt to free my mind from materialism but I do like a Nice GUI for Linux and I'm using it right now.

**"You don't like technical stuff."**

There are a lot of users and each one of them use an OS for a variety of purposes. There are:

1: Programmer
2: Musician
3: Artist
4: Web designer
5: Web surfer
6: Researcher
7: Gamers
8: Others

Programmers have no problem because they are programmers. They work with command-line, read manuals and are thinkers so Linux is for them but there are Musicians/Artists, let's call them Artists. Artists are right-brainers, they are not good at technical stuff. Yes they can copy and paste the commands but what if the command fails? They then will have to research and start reading/experimenting to find out but Artists don't like that. They just want an OS with all of their softwares installed and then get right down to business that's why a lot of them are on Windows/Mac.

If you are lucky you will have everything detected *(Soundcard, videocard, internet, monitor, keyboard, mouse, printer, scanner ect..)* and you are well on your way but for most people that's not the case.

Ubuntu is the only other operating system I’ve looked at seriously for use on my laptop.  I downloaded the Live CD and it looked great, although I didn’t put it through any real workout.  As a computer user only accustomed to Windows and old versions of Mac OS (my first real home computer was a PowerPC, and both my elementary school and university predominantly uses Macs) I’m hesitant about switching to this strange and scary beast we call Linux. ### Post by 3rdalbum on 2006-08-08
> **smaczylo said:**
> I've heard that Ubuntu has problems with NTFS, so should I make the Windows partition FAT32?

Yes, it would be a good idea to make the Windows partition Fat32 if you can manage it. However, Linux is only a stonesthrow away from reliable NTFS writing which is quite exciting.

> **smaczylo said:**
> - My first major compliment is that I love the evolution mail thing.... an integrated calander that I can post when I have appointments is a great idea.

Yeah, Evolution is great. Have you tried clicking the clock in the top Gnome panel? That actually brings up a little Evolution calendar! Great stuff, I love it.

> **smaczylo said:**
> - I have a Creative Zen Micro mp3 player which comes with software for only Windows I assume.  I like listening to music when travelling to and from work, so this is pretty important to me. I've heard mention of Ipod's working on Ubuntu.  I assume a Zen Micro may work as well?

I don't know about the Micro, but I have heard that there is unofficial Linux software for some of the Creative players. I think they're actually linked from the FAQs of the Creative site; otherwise, check Sourceforge.

> **smaczylo said:**
> 
- There is a media disk slot at the side of my laptop, with a Secure Digital reader among others.  I have a digital camera that I take pictures on and transfer to my laptop. I figure this should work with Ubuntu as well, I’m just hesitant to try it as I have important files on it currently and I don’t want them get deleted.

My integrated card reader works out-of-the-box; Ubuntu even recognised which slots were intended for which cards! The camera might be a problem; my camera doesn't work with *gphoto2* which is the framework for digital camera compatibility. If your camera mounts on your Windows desktop without installing extra drivers, then it will definately work with Ubuntu.

Your files are still safe, in any case.

> **smaczylo said:**
> - As I said I watch a lot of movies and shows on my laptop, and they may be encoded in various containers such as .avi, .mkv, .mp4, .ogg, using h264, xvid, divx, you get the point...I’ve heard mplayer is quite good, should it play whatever I throw at it flawlessly? And is it easy to obtain a codec or something allowing me to play .mp3 files as that is what my music, for the most part is.

Mplayer is quite good, but it won't handle *everything* flawlessly. No Linux media player will, but one doesn't have as much trouble as one would expect. I hear good things about VLC, but the interface is not quite as simple. It's very easy to obtain the codecs for MP3 and many other formats, and you can use proprietry Win32 codecs on Linux.

> **smaczylo said:**
> - From what I’ve seen, OpenOffice is fully/nearly compatible, but are there known, awkward formatting issues/different that would occur if I made a spreadsheet or even database in OpenOffice, and it was read in Word/Excel?

OOo, to my knowledge, can't import Access databases. Or maybe it's just the encrypted ones... I'll leave this to someone who knows a bit better. I haven't observed awkward formatting issues, but they do exist. From what I hear, none of the issues are "show-stoppers", unless you need VBA. Visual Basic for Applications is partially supported in StarOffice, which is a commercial, enterprise-level version of OpenOffice.

> **smaczylo said:**
> - I use a Korean downloading program called clubbox, which does not work with Firefox.  It requires ActiveX which I hear Firefox does not have, since viruses are often transmitted through it.  Well, I really like this program, Can any of you guys think of a way in which I could use this program?

You might be able to install Internet Explorer through WINE, which is a Windows compatibility layer. No idea if ActiveX works with it. Ubuntu's programs tend to have good support for languages other than English, so you might find a good Linux equivilant that has a Korean translation.

> **smaczylo said:**
> - Would it be difficult to switch between Windows and Ubuntu? Some university software for textbooks/classes only runs off Windows.  Macs are not supported, and I doubt Linux would be.  I suppose I would need Windows on my computer to access these.

Switching between Windows and Ubuntu on boot-up is easy; there's a tiny program that Ubuntu will install that will start on power-on which gives you the ability to choose which OS you want to run. Some simple software might run on WINE; otherwise, you can always dual-boot or run Windows within VMWare.

> **smaczylo said:**
> - My HP printer/scanner

HP multi-function peripherals have Linux drivers, and these are included with Ubuntu. You'll be glad to find that there's no silly bloated software with it :-)

Dunno if that particular model is covered by the driver, but let's just hope it is :-) You could also check out [www.linuxprinting.org](www.linuxprinting.org) for compatibility information and driver downloads.

### Post by puppy on 2006-08-29
Hmm, well I think I have to say no. I've been unable (after reading pages and pages of posts) to get my digital TV card to work properly and output a picture that's watchable. Same with my Creative Zen Touch which still won't work with Gnomad2. 

I know it's my fault for having components that aren't just plug and play in linux, which many TV cards/mp3 players actually are, but that's what I bought before I decided to install linux. I'm just sick of spending hours and hours trying to get them to work to no avail when I could have been doing something productive. 

I go through this exercise every time new distros are available (it's been Ubuntu the last few times) and I get a little further each time, but I still don't have a working system.

My perception is that linux developers seem to spend a lot of their time writing 15 programs that do the same thing (look at the number of music players out there, with more being added every day...) whilst ignoring the things that will be real showstoppers for people coming to linux from Windows. For instance, I installed  Dapper a few days ago, and in addition to the problems stated above, plugging in my standard USB thumbdrive threw an immediate error. What is a linux noob to make of that?

I didn't post here for help - I'll come back and have a look at whether the next Ubuntu distro meets my needs I guess, but at present it doesn't, but I really wish it did. I know a lot of people come to linux with enthusiasm but then shamble away disillusioned. It's a shame that I have to be one of them yet again  :(

So I installed the laser in Ubuntu, and all I need is to find the right drivers for the inkjet (the model no. isn't listed on the Brother Solutions site) and I am confident I can have both running together in Ubuntu...giving the choice of laser and color.

### Post by confusedipated on 2007-01-03
Well, not to be a downer, but I don't think Ubuntu is for me.  It's not that I mind the command line (part of what I get paid for is to write relatively complex command scripts for Windows).  It's that since installing Ubuntu, everything has been a drag.

I've been around computers for a very long time, and I think I have a pretty good feel for what should come easily, and what can be expected to take effort.  But here's one example: I right clicked the clock, and selected the option to synchronize with NTP servers.  I got a prompt saying that NTP support is not installed.  Select the option to install NTP support, and get prompted for a password.  Enter password, click OK...  The dialog closes, and nothing further happens.  No prompt, no error--nothing.  Repeat entire process to ensure I wasn't dreaming.

Fast forward through half an hour of hair-pulling...  Finally insert the ISO disc for some random reason, and get the package manager.  And there, gee whiz, it says that NTP is installed already.  Huh?  Go back, same circular dance with the NTP sync option.

It still doesn't work, and what's more, I did set the clock correctly when installing Ubuntu.  Why it jumped back to GMT is a mystery for the ages.

And that's just one example.  There are others.  Such as--and I know this'll give you a tickle--figuring out how on earth to access a "command prompt".  Every doc I found that mentioned it simply said to "Open a command prompt..." without saying *how* to do that.  I can't remember how I figured out that it was called *Terminal*.  (I'm not sure what the reasoning is there, because surely no one thinks that "Terminal" is more intuitive than "Command Prompt"...especially since the docs so often refer to the latter term.  I guess it was done just to be different for the sake of difference.  Kind of like a square steering wheel.)

I know, I'm a bastard, I'm a moron, I deserve to be stoned and burned to death simultaneously, I should and *will* burn in hell...  *[Can you tell I've posted in Linux forums before?]*  I guess I'll give Mepis a try, but my expectations are duly low.

---

### Post by whitefort on 2007-01-22
This was a great article, if a little painful for me to read - I tend not to post much on Linux boards because I KNOW I'm probably the kind of Windows migrant who makes accomplished Linux users want to bang their heads against the walls and strangle kittens.

I really, REALLY want to like Linux/Ubuntu. I love the whole philosophy behind Open Source.  But would it be fair to say that the way Linux is sometimes promoted is itself partly a cause of frustration in those trying to switch from Windows?

Just to take a personal example - I bought and read the 'Official Ubuntu Book' before installing Ubuntu.  If you believe the book, the whole thing is pretty much plug 'n' play.  Ubuntu will easily install, effortlessly detect your network printer, you'll click a few buttons and your network will be up and running, etc, etc,.

But really, for an awful lot of people, it's not like that, is it?  It comes down to digging into the innards, changing conf files and so on...  Scary stuff, for the average windows user.

Linux as an OS isn't like buying a new car that you can just turn the key and drive away in - it's more like (in some ways) getting a 'fixer upper', where you're going to have to dig into the engine, do some rewiring, etc before you get a vehicle you can drive around in.  OK, that's not necessarily a bad thing, and the person who fixes it up will learn a LOT more about their vehicle - and maybe even have a lot of fun in the process.

But my point is, if he gets the 'fixer upper' thinking he's getting a car he can just drive off in, there's going to be some frustration, anger, bewilderment...  When a Linux distro is promoted as a viable windows replacement, then the user finds they're going to learn a heck of a lot more about their PC before they even get some basic things running... well, it's a recipe for lots of hard feelings on both sides.

This is why I think Aysiu's article should be compulsory reading for every Windows user who's thinking of making the switch.

For myself, Linux/Ubuntu is something of a love/hate relationship at the moment. We've considered divorce, but I think we're going to keep trying to make it work.  Maybe one day I'll actually find a way to get the network and printer working properly. At present, it means using Windows for actually getting stuff done and Linux for... learning how to make Linux work.

Sorry - I didn't mean to say so much - but it was a great article.

---

The past couple of weeks as been like a love afair with a mistress named Ubuntu; I've slept at my workstation waiting for downloads, Googled for answers and did updates with all the titlating anticipation of a new love.

I googled the wireless installs and most eagerly used the cut and paste feature to bumble through the fix.  Now, I want to watch DVD's on my workstation.

What I REALLY, REALLY want to do is learn to us virtualization to run my loan origination software on LINUX and then to run the server version and then I'm done with Windows.

Thoughout my life, I've had a propensity for stumbling accross up and coming trends and based upon about 50 years of personal life experience (some of which was at AMD and Motorola during my high tech years), I can say that my gut instinct is telling me Ubuntu is going to take LINUX mainstream.  Critical mass is almost achieved. :) 

I'm going to be on the forums asking for help in a) learning to manipulate the operating system via command lines b) installing software c) pulling the shades down on Windows for my business

Thank you in advance to anyone who is able to assist me and I'll do my best to share what I learn with others in a like manner.

After investing over $25,000 of my own money into servers/workstations, software, virus induced tech support, Ubuntu LINUX is a breath of fresh air.

Best regards,

Rocky H
Austin, TX

### Post by rodrigo juarez on 2007-05-07
Problem is that as long as Windows keep having the vast majority of users, developers will continue to create its closed source and pretty expensive software only for it. In time the need for that software will keep the users from change it's OS in light of the amounts of money they have already trowed in software.
Easy example? Flash.
You'll pay 500 to 600 US$ for it. It's not like you'll trow it away just because you want to switch to Linux.
The same way, having that software will make you buy the next Windows version just to keep using it.
It's a vicious circle that needs, at some point, to be broken to stop wasting your money, although there could be some loss in the software you already have, it could ever happen that someone manages to get it working in Linux some day.

---

### Post by aysiu on 2007-05-07
It is a vicious circle, but eventually you reach that critical mass.

Just look at Firefox.

When I first started using it in 2004, it was pretty much hit-or-miss as far as website compatibility. Now, Firefox works with almost every website I visit. I haven't seen the dreaded "you need IE on Windows" message on a website for over half a year. It used to be that sites didn't care about Firefox compatibility because Firefox was about 5% of the browser market. Now, in Europe, it's up to 25%.

If you design a website now with only IE compatibility, you're stupid and losing a lot of potential customers/visitors.

That's how it'll be with desktop Linux in a few years, too.

P.S. Not everyone needs Flash. In fact, the only ones I know who own it are designers who wouldn't use anything besides OS X.

---

### Post by superstar on 2007-05-11
> **aysiu said:**
> In light of a recent thread by a user who felt misled by Linux zealots, I thought it appropriate to create a thread to avoid misleading potential users. I'm telling it straight here.

I'm talking to you, potential user. You've used Windows almost all your life. You think you know a little bit about computers. Maybe you know a lot... about Windows computers. Thought you'd give the most popular distro on [DistroWatch](http://distrowatch.com/dwres.php?resource=major) a chance, eh? Well, let's see if Ubuntu's the right choice for you.

**Hardware**
First of all, keep in mind that any OS will have its hardware limitations. People have endless arguments about why Linux doesn't support absolutely everything. It does come close, but there are some gaps. I know that wireless can be a toughie. I've heard Lexmark printers are almost a no-go. I'd say in the vast majority of cases, you'd be surprised at how much Ubuntu does recognize without any configuring, but you may end up with too low a screen resolution or something.

General distro rule of thumb: if the distro doesn't recognize more than two of your hardware pieces (say, the internet, sound, and screen resolution), you've got the wrong distro. Don't fight it. You can if you want to, but it'll be frustrating. Just get a new distro. Libranet was a pain in my *** when I tried to install it--no internet, weird screen resolution... it wouldn't even recognize my mouse. I tried Ubuntu and only my screen resolution was off. I tried Mepis, and it recognized everything.

**Software**
There are many Linux equivalents for Windows software, but not for everything. Some Windows programs can be run in Linux with some helper applications, but some programs are just hopeless. Find out. Ask questions. If you think you have some Windows-only programs with no Linux substitutes, pop a question into a forum, "I use Macromedia Shockwave. Is there a way to get this working in Linux?" Don't expect too much (Linux can't run everything Windows has), but don't expect too little either (Linux often has more variety, and some programs are even better in Linux), especially if you have money for Cedega or Crossover Office.

**Interface**
[Can you copy and paste instructions?](http://www.ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=59334) I mean literally copy... and paste? You can't? You hate the command line? You'd never want to even touch the command line once? You don't have any frends who are willing to install Ubuntu for you? Okay. If you answered no, no, yes, yes, and yes to those questions, then Ubuntu is not for you. You're probably better off with Mepis or Linspire.

If, however, you embrace the command-line *just for setting up Ubuntu* you'll realize it's easy to copy and paste stuff that people tell you to type in, and that once you've got things set up, you can graphical user interface most of the regular programs (email, internet, etc.).

**Free Price**
Does the lack of having to pay for something (software, OS, or even shipping) appeal to you? Maybe Ubuntu is for you. A lot of other Linux distros will be free but have a more up-to-date edition you pay for, or some subscription that gives you a little more. Ubuntu is free, free, free--no money for anything. Of course, if you want to donate and you have the money, go for it.

**Free Software**
The software is also not proprietary. In practical terms that means you don't have to enter an activation code or limit how many computers you can install the software on. And if you're a programmer, you can even modify the code. However, you should realize that a lot of what you consider "basic" may, in fact, be proprietary. If you want Java support and MP3 support and DVD codecs right away, and you're not willing to copy and paste some simple commands to get them up and running, Ubuntu is not for you. Blag and Mepis have some pretty good out-of-the-box proprietary formats support.

**Community**
Ubuntu's community is amazing. We even put up with trolls and Linux-haters. You'll find the vast majority of users here are knowledgeable, supportive, responsive, and patient. There are a lot of HowTo's as well and some [good links](http://www.ubuntuguide.org).

**Not Windows**
Do you want an OS that's Windows without the spyware? Linux isn't for you. [Linux is not Windows](http://linux.oneandoneis2.org/LNW.htm). Linux can perform tasks that Windows can perform and even more, but it is not Windows. If you can't come with an open mind to Linux, then don't come at all. It's kind of like going from Windows to Mac. There's a learning curve. If you don't have time to learn, just cough up some money for anti-virus and learn more about securing your Windows computer. I've done it. It's not that hard.

I know this generally sounds like a downer. I say use Mepis. I say use Windows. I say something may not work, or you may have to do a lot of copying and pasting to get something working. I just want to warn you. I don't want you to come in with weird expectations about Ubuntu. Please don't twist what's basically a marketing slogan ("Linux for Human Beings") to mean that just about anyone can actually install Ubuntu on any computer and not have to learn anything to get it working. You can't even say that about Windows. If you want to complain about the first thing that goes wrong, don't even bother, because I know you'll complain even if nothing goes wrong. You have to have a positive attitude.

However, if you can get past all that and say, "Yes, I do want a free OS. I don't mind copying and pasting a few commands in the beginning. I want something stable and a little bit different. I don't mind learning things and being part of a supportive and fun community," then dive in! The water is nice. Ubuntu can be a wonderful experience. I used Windows for twenty years, Mac for six years, and Linux for four months (tried more than twelve distros), and here I am using Ubuntu.

P.S. - try it out
**You don't have to reformat your hard drive to give Ubuntu a try. There's a live CD that won't affect your Windows installation** (From the Ubuntu CD case: *Note that a Live CD is much slower than the fully installed Ubuntu system, but allows you to test Ubuntu without affecting your existing software*). You can also set up a dual-boot whereby you can choose whether to boot into Windows or Ubuntu.

P.P.S. If you're not a Ubuntu expert, don't do the expert install. Do the regular install.

P.P.P.S. If you don't have internet access (or have dial-up and little patience with hours of downloading), then please don't use Ubuntu. Software installation and updates will be painful if not impossible.

I first had some trouble after installing Ubuntu, but after a few days trying to learn linux commands i just gave up windows.Cause yes, windows suck. :))

---

### Post by 504harry on 2007-06-11
Hi guys...Is Ubuntu for you?...could have been written for me, esp. with my Lexmark issues...still I have had no difficulty installing Ububtu Live CD and not-much with installing from 5.04 up to 6.06 (the LTS version)...but there are issues that need to be addressed which have little to do with the "Newbie" and much to do with Ubuntu (and Linux in particular) being unhelpful to those who just want to go about their normal work.
After all, that's what they bought the PC for and if Ubuntu is to address the poor peoples in the World it needs to be easier to set-up.
The Lexmark printer issue is not fixed - Lexmark are hand-in-hand with Microsoft so they only appear to supply Windows drivers and where there is a HP emulation this doesn't appear to be detected and doesn't appear to work too well if ever.( mine never has).
Then there was PDF's - those "printed" data sheets that stop you messing about...great idea and Now Ubuntu comes with a PDF-viewer for Firefox...great.
The issue of Java is not resolved - to read Streaming Prices you need "Java" but it is unclear what release is right, as my Win98SE works OK with the supplied (old) Java...but newer version mess up IE. Now, Firefox can download from Sun website, but it offers four Linux-versions - just how am I expected to know which one? I can avoid putting diesel in the petrol (US: gas) filler but some folks don't. I have tried each Java in turn and not one appears to make it through to Firefox....goodness knows where it goes and the same goes for Flash - although if I could disable it, that would be better still. Annoyingly Firefox still complains that Flash is not present (it must have had enough of Java), and this causes the page text to jump about whenthe message appears - then it goes away and the page moves again. Grr.

However, for me the killer issue is partitioning
......I resist the idea of reloadoing Windows to give Ubuntu some room - HDDs are cheap enough so I provide a free-disk. Trying to fire-up the PC, format and partition it using QParted appears to be a recepie for disater IMHO. I tried with earlier releases and it worked so well I got too clever and tried to set aside a data-area for my photos - big mistake, as now I have nearly 56G which it seems cannot be addressed...several Linux people have looked at it but it's still not resolved. The partitioning tool needs to offer a far clearer view of what's happening....so that the drive is arranged "virtually" and easily checked, them set to be partitioned. 
I fail to understand the various extensions...what's wrong with an explanation of the format, if it's so important to make Linux work?
As I may have said I don't think the question should be: "Are you ready for the command-line?" - rather "...can Ubuntu overcome some rather obscure workings to eliminate  the set-up agro".
- The command line survives only because no-one has thought to eliminate the need for it. When you drive your car you just switch on....and it works...there is no reason why the computing power in a PC cannot be used to set-up Ubuntu without the agro....one day it will search out your hardware, make suggestions which you will Agree/Disagree and then it will give a result
----"you need to .... before including Ubuntu2020". 
- and with that,  it will work first time......why not aim for that?....
Although I have to admit the idea of that Article is great...very practical.....yet Ubuntu has moved on....PDF's now included...Goody! So much change in just 6 months. 
Thanks Ubuntu.

### Post by dsrs on 2007-08-27
I just installed feisty fawn today on an old desktop. Success and it looks nice. But no way would it install on my newer computer with two SATA drives in Raid 0 configuration.

As a windoz user all my life I did find the install somewhat intimidating, especially the manual formatting of the hard disk. What sorely disappointed me was that with the live cd I could not write to any of my ntfs hard disks.

Googling showed me the --fairly easy-- way to access and write to ntfs disks in the fully installed Ubuntu, which is great. But I still cannot see/write to any of the network computers in my home, though I tried several suggestions I found in various postings.

This is a major drawback for someone who does not want to abandon an existing windoz setup.

Also, a crucial point. Many posts trumpet the advantage of Linux as being free. Indeed. But the fact is XP and even Vista are also free. I have never paid a penny to Microsoft, except when the OS arrived on a new computer [and then I took it off]. Ever hear of ThePirateBay?

### Post by dondad on 2007-09-17
Great post. I am one of the newbies (April or so of this year) and this forum has been the greatest thing since sliced bread for getting up and running. I haven't gotten answers to everything, but I always put it down that no one read it that had the problem and therefore didn't know how to solve it. Sometimes I could fix it with searches, sometimes with playing around (and sometimes breaking things ;-)) until I figured it out. When I do, I try to post back to my original thread about what I did so that it might help someone else. 

I have tried linux a couple of times before, the latest (before Ubuntu) being xandros, but it had too many tweaks and proprietary stuff in it and I could only install what they released and be fairly sure it would not break things.  What finally pushed me over was Vista. The more I read about it, the less I wanted anything to do with it. I loaded Ubuntu fiesty in a dual boot configuration and started playing. I figured that I had a year or so to learn it before MS stopped supporting xp. After awhile, I found that I was never using xp so I dumped it totally and went strictly Ubuntu. I liked it and was due for a new computer so I bought a Dell xps410 because I knew that the hardware was at least compatible with linux. I believe that trying to make sure that your hardware is supported is one of the major items that can affect your take on linux. I repartitioned to put my home directory on a separate partition, then reinstalled Ubuntu from the CD because I wasn't sure what their recovery would think of  the new partitioning. Right now, things are running fine. I have a few problems on the gutsy installation (dual booting with fiesty), but I expect that as it is still beta software and I am still trying to learn my way around. I have learned a lot trying to get stuff running in the gutsy install without causing problems for myself in the main fiesty setup. Great stuff, one to use, one to play ;-))

It is definitely a learning curve. I have been using windows siince version 1.0. Went to windows 286, then windows 386, then 3.0, then 3.1, then 3.11 for workgroups, then 95, was a beta tester for my company on 98, then xp. In another life I wrote all of the assembly code for prototypes at my company and later was the manager of an oem software development group. (yes, I am an old f*** ;-)) With this, I had a fair idea what I was in for, but it has been a good ride. I have no plans to ever go back to Windows at home (I have to use it at work) and I think this forum and others for making this possible.

### Post by Irihapeti on 2007-09-21
I saw the Ubuntu liveCD offered with NZ PCWorld magazine in the local supermarket, and figured it was worth the $8 NZ for the magazine in order to have a look.

I thought Ubuntu was going to be something I just played with for a little while, to satisfy my curiosity, and I'd then go back to using Windows full time. 

Yes, my curiosity has been satisfied, but I was completely and utterly WRONG on the latter point.

I've struck a few problems along the way, all of which I've been able to solve - largely with the help of this forum - and now I can do everything I want to do in Ubuntu. Having said that, I'm aware that although it meets MY needs, it won't necessarily meet everyone's.

So Windows is in dire and imminent danger of getting the boot from my machine. As in formatting /dev/sda1 to ext3. I think it comes down to my getting around to doing it, which might mean when I find I desperately need the space. (e.g. video files)

Just as an aside, I'd like to mention that it's not only Ubuntu users who have video and sound driver problems. I was talking to my son last night - he's had ongoing problems with XP and his Acer laptop.

And same son has just discovered spyware/trojan problems... I shall take the opportunity to gently encourage considering options other than better antispyware programs.

Irihapeti

---

### Post by aysiu on 2007-09-21
> **blackcp said:**
> I thought Linspire was like windows without  spyware? =P In other words windows clone with a linux kernel.
Not exactly. Linspire is Linux in Windows clothing. It isn't Windows, though. It cannot run Windows programs any better than other Linux distributions, and it doesn't function in other respects like Windows (registry, different folder hierarchy). It's basically KDE made to look as much like Windows as possible, plus a few renamed applications.

---

### Post by aysiu on 2007-10-02
That's very encouraging to hear, James. I'm a firm believer in not overhyping Ubuntu. If you're realistic and set people's expectations low, people usually end up pleasantly surprised and motivated to rise to the challenge. If you make it sound as if Ubuntu is the cat's pajamas and also a free version of Windows that's completely trouble-free, you'll just get frustrated users who feel misled.

Thanks for giving Ubuntu and its community a second chance!

### Post by Bruce M. on 2007-10-07
Is Ubuntu for "me"?  - YES!
  A couple of phrases come to mind:

  Better the devil you know then the one you don't.
  and
  Man's biggest fear is the fear of the unknown.

  Well, for years I was stuck with the devil OS I knew, and feared the unknown OS (Linux).
  In an email I said: I've been thinking about Linux, but something has always stopped me from taking the plunge.
  And got the reply: The recent Ubuntu series is much user friendly to use and linux is adequate for normal work. I only kept XP for games.
  I'm not a gamer, and shortly after this "the devil I knew" crashed 3 times in as many weeks.
  I made the move. I had my computer download the Live CD for 6.06 (because 7.04 didn't seem to want to come) and requested a Live CD by mail. The next morning I had 6.06 running with W2K quite nicely.  I didn't have to use my Intel Motherboard Setup CD, I didn't have use my Samsung SyncMaster Setup CD, I didn't have to use my Epson Printer Setup CD, everything configured in quite nicelt thank you.  What a bonus!
  I kept my mind open and plunged in.  Made a lot of mistakes that meant re-installing W2K a few times along with Ubuntu a few times as well.
  The Live CD (7.04) I received by mail was defective and that created a headache or two so I finally downloaded the Alternate CD (7.04) and installed that.
  As to the learning curve.  Isn't LIFE a learning curve?
  I like the NO viruses, no firewall, no anti-malware software, and as an added bonus, the price: FREE!  I'm also pushing it with my friends as well.
  I "love" the Launchpad Question area.  More then half of my questions were never asked, because I read the threads where others had asked the same question before me.
  The people in the Ubuntu community that help out are GREAT to say the least.
  OK, I've added my piece, and feel better.

### Post by Ryangarve on 2007-10-24
A good rule of thumb is if you had trouble setting up windows ubuntu is not for you.  I consider myself knowledgeable with computers, but I had one heck of a time trying to get hibernate working and my laptop still boots up very slow.  Also just the basic setup of partition your hard and setting up a swap drive can be a chore without prior knowledge.  Don't get me wrong I love unbuntu, but it isn't the ultimate and it isn't for everyone.  Take it for what it is, try it out on the live cd and if you think the install won't be to tough give it a try.

I do wish my games would work though ;( but i also haven't tried.

  Anyway long babble short..... Linux is a great OS if you want to learn and you have google access.  I like that it grows with me and the more i learn the better it works.  Its been 4 days with Ubuntu and I'm already more productive with it than windows.... And for some reason command line just seams to make more sense than a GUI half the time... wierd... now if i could just get down the whole whats in what dir thing /etc/ /bin/ bla bla bla ill get there.. one day 


p.s.  | grep is my friend

MAC OS

search returned all kinds of results, Mac OS X for all these different cat names, like Lions, Tigers and Bears, oh my! Out of all the $100 OS's, one stood out:

"An alternative operating system for your Mac!" 

The starting bid was $9.99 or somewhere around that. OK Cool. clicked on it. Mentioned were all these features, games, applications, I was intrigued. (I really don't recall the description said anything about "Linux" or "Ubuntu," either. Maybe it did, but i don't remember seeing any, all I cared about was getting this machine to work!) ### Post by soleille on 2008-02-19
YUP, it definitely is :o) I use it about 90% of the time now

I'm fairly (windows) computer savvy, but NOT a tinkerer and the command line frightened me to hell and back - I've been running Ubuntu for a month or two now and haven't ever had to use it except to connect to the internet, but that's because of the weird university network setup (point and click client for win machines, ssh2 client for linux - I already managed to set up an alias so I don't have to type the whole command hehe)
(I had Ubuntu installed for me, so I'm talking about *using* it without the CL)

I LOVE how even the most obscure hardware instantly worked (haven't yet tried my Lexmark printer/scanner yet though, but I hardly ever need it anyway, it wasn't installed on my 2 recent windows installations either)

Now there are only 3 reasons why I sometimes use my windows partition:
1) I haven't been motivated enough to tinker with the BIOS age cutoff problem apparently common on Thinkpads, and sometimes Ubuntu doesn't start off because of that
2) Nothing beats reading books on Microsoft Reader (no luck running this through either wine or crossover)
3) Working in a windows only internet café, it's good to stay familiar with where which button is :o)

The only thing about the switch that I haven't gotten used to by now is the different path names - I still want to type  c:/my computer/...whatever - but I'm pretty sure learning *that* way took me longer than the Ubuntu way will.

The rest is about as easily useable or, in many cases, better than Windows in my opinion- and we're looking at someone who's been using windows for about 50 hours/week and is a creature of habit - no real problems here.

Also, I was thrilled by how helpful people in this forum are. I especially liked how people managed to basically walk me through baby steps without making me feel like a complete ignoramus who hasn't quite grasped the ABC- I've never found this combination in windows forums and, as someone who sometimes teaches languages, know just what kind of  a gift it is to be able to explain in such a way - I definitely don't have it.
And newbies wanting help on absolute beginner level really just should mention themselves just how much of a noob they are if they want help at their level - Ubuntu comes with a lot, but not with a crystal ball yet!
