### Post by jross61 on 2008-07-08
> **aysiu said:**
> In light of a recent thread by a user who felt misled by Linux zealots, I thought it appropriate to create a thread to avoid misleading potential users. I'm telling it straight here.

I'm talking to you, potential user. You've used Windows almost all your life. You think you know a little bit about computers. Maybe you know a lot... about Windows computers. Thought you'd give the most popular distro on [DistroWatch](http://distrowatch.com/dwres.php?resource=major) a chance, eh? Well, let's see if Ubuntu's the right choice for you.

**Hardware**
First of all, keep in mind that any OS will have its hardware limitations. People have endless arguments about why Linux doesn't support absolutely everything. It does come close, but there are some gaps. I know that wireless can be a toughie. I've heard Lexmark printers are almost a no-go. I'd say in the vast majority of cases, you'd be surprised at how much Ubuntu does recognize without any configuring, but you may end up with too low a screen resolution or something.

General distro rule of thumb: if the distro doesn't recognize more than two of your hardware pieces (say, the internet, sound, and screen resolution), you've got the wrong distro. Don't fight it. You can if you want to, but it'll be frustrating. Just get a new distro. Libranet was a pain in my *** when I tried to install it--no internet, weird screen resolution... it wouldn't even recognize my mouse. I tried Ubuntu and only my screen resolution was off. I tried Mepis, and it recognized everything.

**Software**
There are many [Linux equivalents for Windows software](http://www.linuxappfinder.com), but not for everything. Some Windows programs can be run in Linux with some helper applications, but some programs are just hopeless. Find out. Ask questions. If you think you have some Windows-only programs with no Linux substitutes, pop a question into a forum, "I use Macromedia Shockwave. Is there a way to get this working in Linux?" Don't expect too much (Linux can't run everything Windows has), but don't expect too little either (Linux often has more variety, and some programs are even better in Linux), especially if you have money for Cedega or Crossover Office.

**Interface**
[Can you copy and paste instructions?](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?p=315402&highlight=user-friendly#post315402) I mean literally copy... and paste? You can't? You hate the command line? You'd never want to even touch the command line once? You don't have any frends who are willing to install Ubuntu for you? Okay. If you answered no, no, yes, yes, and yes to those questions, then Ubuntu is not for you. You're probably better off with Mepis or Linspire.

If, however, you embrace the command-line *just for setting up Ubuntu* you'll realize it's easy to copy and paste stuff that people tell you to type in, and that once you've got things set up, you can graphical user interface most of the regular programs (email, internet, etc.).

**Free Price**
Does the lack of having to pay for something (software, OS, or even shipping) appeal to you? Maybe Ubuntu is for you. A lot of other Linux distros will be free but have a more up-to-date edition you pay for, or some subscription that gives you a little more. Ubuntu is free, free, free--no money for anything. Of course, if you want to donate and you have the money, go for it.

**Free Software**
The software is also not proprietary. In practical terms that means you don't have to enter an activation code or limit how many computers you can install the software on. And if you're a programmer, you can even modify the code. However, you should realize that a lot of what you consider "basic" may, in fact, be proprietary. If you want Java support and MP3 support and DVD codecs right away, and you're not willing to copy and paste some simple commands to get them up and running, Ubuntu is not for you. Blag and Mepis have some pretty good out-of-the-box proprietary formats support.

**Community**
Ubuntu's community is amazing. We even put up with trolls and Linux-haters. You'll find the vast majority of users here are knowledgeable, supportive, responsive, and patient. There are a lot of HowTo's as well and some [good links](http://www.ubuntuguide.org).

**Not Windows**
Do you want an OS that's Windows without the spyware? Linux isn't for you. [Linux is not Windows](http://linux.oneandoneis2.org/LNW.htm). Linux can perform tasks that Windows can perform and even more, but it is not Windows. If you can't come with an open mind to Linux, then don't come at all. It's kind of like going from Windows to Mac. There's a learning curve. If you don't have time to learn, just cough up some money for anti-virus and learn more about securing your Windows computer. I've done it. It's not that hard.

I know this generally sounds like a downer. I say use Mepis. I say use Windows. I say something may not work, or you may have to do a lot of copying and pasting to get something working. I just want to warn you. I don't want you to come in with weird expectations about Ubuntu. Please don't twist what's basically a marketing slogan ("Linux for Human Beings") to mean that just about anyone can actually install Ubuntu on any computer and not have to learn anything to get it working. You can't even say that about Windows. If you want to complain about the first thing that goes wrong, don't even bother, because I know you'll complain even if nothing goes wrong. You have to have a positive attitude.

However, if you can get past all that and say, "Yes, I do want a free OS. I don't mind copying and pasting a few commands in the beginning. I want something stable and a little bit different. I don't mind learning things and being part of a supportive and fun community," then dive in! The water is nice. Ubuntu can be a wonderful experience. I used Windows for twenty years, Mac for six years, and Linux for four months (tried more than twelve distros), and here I am using Ubuntu.

P.S. - try it out
**You don't have to reformat your hard drive to give Ubuntu a try. There's a live CD that won't affect your Windows installation** (From the Ubuntu CD case: *Note that a Live CD is much slower than the fully installed Ubuntu system, but allows you to test Ubuntu without affecting your existing software*). You can also set up a dual-boot whereby you can choose whether to boot into Windows or Ubuntu.

P.P.S. If you don't have internet access (or have dial-up and little patience with hours of downloading), then please don't use Ubuntu. Software installation and updates will be painful if not impossible.

P.P.P.S. Other distros that include popular proprietary stuff and more graphical tools "out of the box" are PCLinuxOS, Linux Mint, and Sabayon.
As someone who dos'nt know a lot about computers,I found ubuntu to easy to learn,very easy to use after reading the howto's on the ubuntu forums,keep up the great work

---

### Post by michael1234 on 2008-08-26
**_Let me tell you all a little story..._**

First of all, YES, Ubuntu is definitely for me. [COLOR="Blue"]I have gone from being content with Windows, to liking Linux and still being ok with Windows, to being totally anti-Windows and pro-GNU/Linux.[/COLOR] Here's why:

I have used Windows since the days of 3.1, when everything was either Program Manager or DOS. I cut my teeth on Win, learned Win, learned MS-DOS (in the process of rescuing my broken Win systems 10 times because I didn't know what I was doing) and got to a point where I knew the basics of Windows so well that I could tutor people in how to maintain their systems.

When Vista came out and my wife and I got a 3rd computer, I knew I was ready to try out Linux. Ubuntu was the easy pick because it is so well known and supported and the GUI looked easy enough to use.

So I shrank the NTFS partition on our laptop, made a new ext3 partition, and installed Ubuntu. [COLOR="DarkGreen"]I was so excited I didn't bother to do a backup...
[U]
***WHOOPS!***[/U]   :shock:

Whoa... none of our Windows-computers came with a restore CD. Screw wading in the water, I was diving in headfirst. I was now "stuck" using Ubuntu![/COLOR] :-$

So I powered on and logged in for the first time. Everything worked, except for the sound and the wireless (argh!) but when I plugged in the ethernet cable I was fine. So I used the wire to get on the Internet and researched my problems. The sound card thing was easy (an "external amp" setting that didn't work on laptops). The wireless dogged me for some time (in fact it [still doesn't work upon login]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=872510"), but now I can get it working in seconds). Besides, I didn't back-up because most of our important files were on our old desktop/external drive connected to it.

**_THIS is where things really got interesting._**

Recently I installed an AGP card in our old system. While doing that, I nudged the cpu fan/heatsink out of place (I have got to be less careless :-() The card was great, but the processor got nuked!!    :oops:

Through luck and shrewd eBay know-how, I got a high-performance used desktop unit (with the AGP slot) for $100 flat. $100!:) A 3.06 Ghz hyperthreaded processor! Even a 10,000 rpm SCSI HDD... which requires a controller driver be loaded before bootup (read on, this is key...)

So the first thing I'm thinking is, now I can use this new superfast SCSI disk for another Linux system and link up the HDD from our old desktop. Ok, cool. So I defrag the preinstalled Windows--twice--shrink NTFS, write an ext3 partition, and throw Kubuntu on there.,,

[COLOR="DarkSlateBlue"]Of course, it doesn't boot.[/COLOR] But this time, the pc came with its own Win xp restore CD, so all should be great, right? Nothing ventured, no backup needed anyway. Plus I was using my Ubuntu laptop from before for fast, clean, checksummed Brasero CD image burning of stuff like Knoppix, Puppy Linux, and other rescue stuff which I had recently learned was handy for system rescues. I was prepared. [COLOR="DarkOliveGreen"]The Win xp restore CD was even custom made ONLY for my Dell computer[/COLOR] (maybe so I wouldn't install "pirate" copies on other PCs). But wouldn't you know it...

...I boot up the Win xp restore cd, and...[COLOR="DarkSlateGray"]IT DOESN'T SEE THE SCSI HARD DISK!![/COLOR]  ](*,)  What's this about? The driver loads with the BIOS! Knoppix and Puppy Linux live CDs could see it, even if Puppy had trouble mounting it. But guess what?? There is a point in the restore process where Windows asks something like, *"If you have a third-party driver [floppy] disk, please insert it now and press F6."* What do you mean? I thought the Win xp restore CD was made just for this PC and ALREADY HAD the driver on it!

[COLOR="DarkRed"]This was the tipping point for me. Right then, I went from being a "cool with Linux, cool with Windows" person to belonging to the "screw Windows, Linux rules" crowd.[/COLOR] How the heck was I supposed to get the driver with no usable OS?

The Win restore CD isn't a live CD. You can't boot into anything like Windows xp from it. You can't even get a GUI from it. It will detect your hardware...but not so you can go on the Internet and look for solutions to your problem, like you can on most Linux live CDs. All you can do is (1) "recover" your system and pray it won't bugger your configuration (2) murder your filesystem and do a clean install, or (3) go to an impotent, stripped-down DOS prompt that allows you to do virtually nothing.

It's like when the police have your car towed to a remote scrap metal yard reachable ONLY by car. Forget about buses or other public transport. You don't have a car and you need a car to GET YOUR CAR BACK! URGH!   :confused:

I decided to take the free taxicab called Knoppix to the yard.

Knoppix loaded up and detected everything. And [COLOR="DarkGreen"]guess what? I got on the Internet and found a Windows floppy-disk-image program to supply the necessary driver that the Win restore CD wanted![/COLOR] Of course the program didn't work in Knoppix, but hey, I could just use the Windows on my work computer to set up the floppy. Turns out I didn't have to wait that long (read on...)

I proceeded to format my whole HDD and used qparted to partition it into ext3 and NTFS. And [COLOR="Indigo"]this time the ext3 partition came FIRST so GRUB would be king[/COLOR] and Kubuntu would boot.

_**Here's the knockout blow:**_    :twisted:

Kubuntu is up. My first time using KDE, and I am LOVING it. But Wine isn't yet good enough to run that Windows floppy disk imager for my SCSI HDD driver...I gotta do that in Windows. I loathe the idea of bringing a floppy to and from work (they are SO easy to mess up!) So then I get a farfetched idea: I'd heard about virtual machines somewhere, but never tried them out...my new machine is certainly powerful enough...

[COLOR="DarkBlue"]Within minutes I am compiling VMware for Debian/Ubuntu. Shoot, I can run Windows as a virtual machine inside Linux? Heck yeah! And wouldn't you know it...No sooner do I get a working Win xp virtual OS going inside VMware, I open up that floppy driver-writing utility and it works!![/COLOR]

So there you have it: I had to use LINUX distros to clean up after Windows' mess. Boy, if all those end-users hooked on Windows out there knew what I know now, there'd be a lot less headaches and temper tantrums, hardware prices would tumble, and customer service departments the world over would have a lot more free time on their hands to deal with more complex problems, leading to faster, better bug fixes everywhere.

Ever since I've been talking to my wife about how (1) she needs to try Linux, (2) she needs to learn Linux, and (3) she needs to eventually migrate with me to a Linux-only environment. No, I'm not a nag... but if the discussion comes up, I am quite clear about my views. Neither of us are gamers. She just uses Second Life, which runs flawlessly on my Kubuntu installation, so that's no problem.

_**The bottom line:**_

If you want to spend an extra hundred or two dollars to get Windows and (almost) never have to see a command line interface, I understand. And as long as you maintain your system ($35/year for antivirus, frequent defrags/disk cleanups, backups, and the like) and it doesn't fail, you should be all right. **Much as I don't like Windows, it's not a complete failure.** I accomplished good stuff on Win operating systems for 14 years; I won't deny that.

[COLOR="DarkRed"]**But what happens when something goes wrong?** If a DLL needed by your beloved MS Office is missing? If the filesystem dies? If your hard disk goes on the fritz? What if, one day, you can't get into good ol' Windows? And you can't wait a few days and spend $50-$100 to get it to a tech to fix? What if you backed up your files but can't save the configuration of Quicken, MS Office, NeatReceipts, <insert proprietary program here>, etc--and you've misplaced the install disks for those programs? What if you can't download antivirus updates because the antivirus company won't let you used the same active serial number twice, even though you reinstalled on the same computer? What if, like many people who buy computers with preinstalled Windows, you have no real Windows install CD and all you can do is fondle the extremely limited system restore CD and pray that it'll be ok? What if it isn't ok? How much money are you willing to pay to get Windows back?[/COLOR]

[COLOR="DarkGreen"]**Well, if you had Linux,** you could keep going with a Live CD (Kubuntu Live CD has OpenOffice, for example) and save to an inexpensive USB flash drive. Go on the Internet, search for solutions to your problem, and hack and learn. As for your configuration? Use the rescue modes, and don't worry--you won't be shoehorned into just 2 or 3 all-or-nothing options. You may feel like you have too many options, but you'll appreciate that when you spend no money, get your stuff back in gear, and learn some really useful stuff about how it all works in the process. Antivirus? by now you know that, although not virus-proof, you are just as good as such when using Linux. Lost your configuration? Need your programs reinstalled? You got a few easy choices: Synaptic, Adept, apt-get, aptitude, etc... and you're back in the saddle at NO CHARGE.[/COLOR]

It's easier to go to a dealership and just pay the sticker price to buy a new car, yes... but do you know what you are getting into? Did you do studies on the car's reliability, the dealer's business record, the terms of your warranty? Do you know how many thousands of dollars you could save if you just had enough knowledge and bargained them down?
[COLOR="DarkSlateBlue"]
**No, Ubuntu isn't for everyone.** Maybe it's not for the people who just walk up and pay the sticker price at the dealership and do little to no research on their purchase. **But** if you are the type who does not want to be held hostage by your own ignorance, and if you would save yourself big car purchase money by bargaining that dealer down with price quotes, stats, and informed questions--then Ubuntu is certainly for you.[/COLOR]

---

### Post by Halbarad on 2008-08-29
Is Ubuntu for me? Yeah, it is. I love its power, its flexibility, its elegance. And I love its philosophy as well, and even the meaning(s) of the word "Ubuntu". But I think that **many more Windows users would migrate to Ubuntu** if they got help during their **_First Radical Crisis_**. The FRC comes during the first or second day and is very bad -- it was very bad to me and I was very near to decide to abandon everything and go back to Windows. 
Just imagine: NN is an experienced Windows user. That means: she knows how to fix problems in windows, how to get things working. Let's say, NN is able to check and create disk partitions in XP following this route: Control Panel-Administration Tools-Resource Management-Disk Management. She knows how to check the hardware she has on her machine, the installed packages, the video codecs etc. Sure, she heard about Linux -- legendary efficiency, no viri, freedom from the megatechnocrat M$. Then she tries Ubuntu from livecd and says: wow, it's not SO different from Windows! I could manage it!!!-- and she goes on with double boot installation.
Pleasant surprises: since NN is connected to a router, network and internet work without need to configure anything; most of her hardware, all the relevant stuff, is recognized and properly configured. But, unless she is extremely lucky, something goes wrong sooner or later (say, wireless does not work, something is wrong with the screen.) Or she just wants to check her drivers, or to have a look at her disk. The radical crisis is approaching. Her first thought is: let's see in which way the hardware is configured, what is recognized as what, which drivers are on... and in a few, nightmarish minutes, after some hectic double-clicking in the desperate search of control panels, resource management or whatever, NN suddenly realizes the **devastating truth**: in order to get the most trivial info on her system she has to go through a new and extremely complex system of commands; has to find her way in an ocean of help documentation in which she can find anything of use only after painful and disorderly research. She goes on for a while copying lines she doesn't understand -- with an increasing, overwhelming feeling of blindness and inadequacy. And -- she thinks -- even if I could find out about peripherals and drivers, I don't have the least idea how I could set up a new driver, where to look for a program, what an exe file corresponds to... gosh, maybe there is *nothing* that corresponds to an exe file in this heap of files!!! She spends half an hour to find out how to type such exotic characters as '`' (or she gives up and does just copy and paste). She goes on, opens interface files, she finds out that many essential data are in a folder called *"etc"*! And not "ETC" or "Etc", but just "etc"! Is *this* the legendary order and flexibility of Linux?!?  Eventually, a statement begins to flash to her mind, as a bottom line: "Linux is good for people who have nothing to do but learning a family of idiosyncratic programming languages: people who enjoy to spend their time fixing tricky problems or taking part in inconclusive forums. But I can't devote my life to Linux learning!!!"
Maybe I have depicted it too dramatically: at any rate, my radical crisis was not this bad (it **almost** was, though). But many people I know had reactions of this kind. Fortunately, I am very stubborn, very much attracted to Linux and much disappointed by Windows in practically all respects. But the decisive point is: I installed Ubuntu while I was on holiday. I had plenty of time for browsing through the documentation and for trying things, taking notes etc.
I have written above that many more Windows users would migrate to Ubuntu if they found more help during the first crisis. This should be edited, though, or rather it should be specified. It is not a matter of how much help one gets. In a way, there is way too much help available for an ordinary user. The recommended documentation for beginners would require one fulltime work year to read, perhaps two years to study.
My proposal: a short guide to terminal commands and basic operations, a :arrow:**_[COLOR="Blue"]Companion for one's first month in Ubuntu[/COLOR]._** Some proposed chapters: getting info on hardware and drivers (that is, lshw, lspci, ifconfig etc.); disks, partitions, free space etc.; packages, how to list, install uninstall and remove; editing files. With short explanations about the system, program folders and structure. The *only aim* of such a guide should be to enable a user to master the basic system functions that an advanced user already masters in another operating system. Since its purpose is to be used for one month, it must: (i) be short (2-3000 words at most); (ii) contain only a few links -- more links are merely bewildering.
I wonder what other Ubuntu users would think about this -- and what suggestions they would make. Or perhaps there is already such a thing, which I was not able to find in the ocean!! :mrgreen:

---

### Post by trickytrickytricky on 2008-10-29
I feel I have to add something here. I started trying to use linux a couple of years ago. I tried Linspire, pclinuxos and a couple of others and then ubuntu - which I am persevering with. Now I am a fairly competent windows user, no slouch, I've recovered crashed systems, carried out new installations, installed new and extra hard disks etc etc. I have an enquiring mind and have often searched out windows answers through my own endeavour and also by asking for help on windows forums.
The problem I have with Ubuntu is that so many of the experienced people who answer the noob questions on the forums seem to like to show off their greater knowledge. Very often it is obvious from the way the question is framed that the enquirer has very little, if any, knowledge of how ubuntu works. Yet they get answers that tell them to build a kernel, sudo something and dev the other ad infinitum.

So it's all very well writing a long article telling us to try something different if it's too much hard work for us, but how about helping us along a little? Tell us where to start for crying out loud! If I say I've just installed ubuntu and I ask how I can get my olivetti printer to work, is there any point in quoting technical gobbledegook at me? Isn't it obvious that I probably won't know what most of the technical terms in ubuntu mean? Advising me to go and install mepis or blag isn't very helpful, is it? 

I have a busy job that precludes me playing about with my computer all day, as much as I'd like to, so my time on here is precious. There's so much knowledge on here but it's not really shared as much as the linux community likes to pretend it is. I'm forced to sit here using trial and error, digging and sifting to try and find out how to build something that will drive my printer, my webcam. Find out myself what sudo and dev mean, because those who know don't seem to want to tell me - I ask and I get a one, two maybe three word reply. Thanks a bundle.

I moved from this forum to another thinking that maybe those with the patience to explain things were elsewhere, but it's the same. And here is something some of you should think about - I've asked for a lot of help and advice on windows forums and I tend to get get helpful, fully informed answers pitched at my obvious level of competence. I was led to believe that the linux community were even better at it. Not so.

If you want the linux community to grow and improve, then you have to let the noobs in on the action. I want to stay with ubuntu, I'm trying very, very hard to turn my back on windows, but I'm also trying very, very hard to master ubuntu - on my own.

Thank you for reading this.

The tricky one.

### Post by aysiu on 2008-10-29
Well, trickytrickytricky, I have to agree with you. Unfortunately, since anyone can sign up for these forums and post "help" on any support thread, there's not much I can do besides trying to model good help.

Let me give you an example.

Quite often a new user will post a thread saying *How do I install Flash for Firefox?*

Within minutes (I'm not kidding), she'll get a flood of responses but most of them will not be helpful ones.

Here is a sampling of what she's likely to get: [quote=Unhelpful response #1]Just do this: ```
sudo apt-get install flashplugin-nonfree
```[/quote] [quote=Unhelpful response #2]Just do this: ```
sudo apt-get install ubuntu-restricted-extras
```[/quote] [quote=Unhelpful response #3]Go to the Adobe website and download the .tar.gz file, unzip it, and run the installer script inside[/quote] The first response is unhelpful because it gives a terminal command instead of a point-and-click method for doing a simple task, and it doesn't even tell the new user where to use the command or that she can copy and paste the command instead of retyping it.

The second response is also unhelpful for the same reasons, but it also doesn't address the issue that the new user wants to install Flash. The *ubuntu-restricted-extras* metapackage also installs Java, MP3 playback, and a bunch of other popular proprietary codecs. Now, granted, it's very probable a new user would want all that stuff as well, but it'd be nice to tell her "This command will install more than just Flash" so she knows what's happening.

The third is unhelpful because it doesn't tell the user how to unzip the .tar.gz file or how to run the script inside. It also makes the process of installing Flash a lot more complicated than it has to be.

Now what I would post is something more like this: [quote=What I think is more helpful as a response]Visit a website requiring Flash and click the *Install Missing Plugins* drop-down button and follow the prompts. You can get a screenshot tutorial walkthrough on this process here: [http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntu/flash](http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntu/flash)

Let us know if you encounter any problems, and we'll try to help you through it.[/quote] I do this for several reasons: [list][*]It is the most direct way to install Flash[*]It requires only point-and-click (no terminal commands)[*]It shows the user that good tutorials exist and lets her know there will be screenshots so that she isn't lost in a sea of text.[/list] I'm not anti-terminal commands. I'm actually a big fan. But for simple tasks, new users should be directed to point-and-click solutions whenever possible. Terminal commands for new users are better for troubleshooting problems.

---

It's not bloated, and doesn't have anti-virus and huge firewalls / spyware stoppers sucking my resources when i need them most.

In summary, I'd like to say this to posters: - 

NEWCOMMERS - Don't give up. It's definitely worth hanging in there. Give it 2 weeks before you say "it's not for me". After this time, I suspect 90 + percent will stick with Linux. Just remember, Windows was hard to get to grips with as a newcommer - probably harder.

HELPERS / ADVICE GIVERS - Please be a little more patient and explicit with your posts. Ex-windozers like me don't know a Sudo from a Sumo, nor where to stick it for that matter. I'm far from stupid, but need the answers to be a little more than 1-line Sudo scripts.

ps I WILL now be recommending Linux to others.

### Post by KnottyMars on 2009-04-08
Im loving Ubuntu, Ive been messing with the OS for about 4 months now and I feel like Im starting to get the hang of things (a little) 

I think Winders has really made people lazy. When you can click and drag almost anything, you have no concept that there are DOS commands happening in the background and you are in fact running commands, but you cant see them. 

With a mixed GUI and command line environment such as Ubuntu, it forces you to operate in some command line items, but with the warm fuzzies of a GUI desktop. Im proud that I was able to install and get a couple Ubuntu systems up and running. Our company is trying to save money anywhere we can and when we can fill in the guest cubicle with Ubuntu and not have to shell out $140 for the OS license alone, its all the better. 

Im also proud that Ive tried to stay true to the command line, Ive faithfully looked up things in the forums before posting for help, Ive also started experimenting with Apache2 PHP and MYSQL, (things that were beyond my ability 4 months ago). 

Thanks to all the folks that made Ubuntu happen. Its not for everyone, but if you start slow and take things one step at a time, you will find a very useful operating system. 

Sorry for the novel, but Im glad that its not an OS for the masses. you need to learn a little to function and that is not a bad thing in these times of instant gratification for everything. ;)

---

### Post by AICollector on 2009-07-04
Disabled (and as a result of that, broke?) 
Check.

Fed up of fighting a loosing battle on Windows against Internet nasties? Check.

Fed up of reinstalling system every four months and spend an entire day putting in drivers and other rubbish?
Check.



I would recommend to those who are thinking of moving to Linux to do what is known as a 'soft migration'; Firefox, GIMP, VLC Media Player, Open Office; get used to some of the software on Windows then it won't be so much of a shock to switch systems.

### Post by silvagroup on 2009-07-04
I have been using a *nix OS for over 12 years here's what I suggest:

That you back up ALL your important data first !!!!!!! Just a precaution the *nix installers are really good, but we are really bad at selecting the wrong option at times.

Then begin with a dual boot system, all the *nix OS's are very good about setting up a dual boot system. Yes there are live cd'd but a live cd doesn't work quite the same once it is installed on your system.

Also try different flavours and spins as you can install as many as you want, until you find the one that really works well with your hardware and for you.

Try it for for a month or so if it's not what you want try and install another over the other or on a different partition if you want.

Then after that process you may want to do a total migration.
For the average user, who just uses email, web browsing, and social networking with standard hardware migration can be fairly simple.

If that's not you, expect to have to spend some time googling to fine tune the *nix system to get everything working.

If you have special Windows only software that you must have, learn about emulators, virtualization  or maintain a dual boot system, and if you need a modem for internet connection or faxing make sure it's not a soft modem, because you will have problems.

Following these recommendations will make your move from Windows to a *nix OS a much more enjoyable transition.

By the way I am currently using Ubuntu Jaunty on all my home systems, Win4linPro as my virtualizer and I have one laptop which still is a dual boot system, I rarely use Windows but there are a few very rare occations when I have to boot into it.

---

### Post by Irihapeti on 2009-07-04
> **silvagroup said:**
> ... if you need a modem for internet connection or faxing make sure it's not a soft modem, because you will have problems.


My own experience is that some soft modems work quite well. I had a D-Link DFM-562IS which has a Conexant chip (well, I still have it, but I'm not using it now) and I used it for 18 months on Ubuntu. So it may be worth trying to get a soft modem going, but to be prepared for the possibility that it won't work.

I'm glad I persisted with this one, because my only other option was to give up and stay with Windows, which isn't something I wanted to do.

### Post by RabbitWho on 2010-03-20
I think if i had read that before downloading it might have discouraged me. Thankfully with my attention span I would never have managed to read it (I'm not saying it's too long, it's my problem). 

For someone like me it would be worth saying this: 

-Most things work equally good and better, most things are faster and easier than windows. 
-When you first download it it will take some time to get used to and find everything. 
-Every now and then it will take you hours and hours to get some new thing to work, if you are the type of person who gives up after a few hours, or gets angry, then don't try it.

### Post by 3Miro on 2011-03-29
Good first post, still relevant despite being old. I have only two comments:

Major: add a line about Virtual Box, if one has only an occasional need for windows (say once a month to print something or access a incompatible MS Office document), then Virtual Box is a great solution. It does require a windows license, but many people have an old XP CD laying around.

Minor: I would switch the points about "free and in cheap" and "free as in freedom". People would have little trouble understanding cheap, but freedom would be new to most seeing Linux for the first time.
